<div id="review_343938638" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/343938638" />
    <a title="Williwaw" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3491750-williwaw"><img alt="Williwaw" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304797883p2/3491750.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/343938638?book_show_action=true">Jun 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Williwaw" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Williwaw" href="/user/show/3491750-williwaw">Williwaw</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780441106257" href="/book/show/898828.City">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer343938638" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7421789750492913166">Why is Clifford Simak virtually a forgotten writer?<br/><br/>"City" won the International Fantasy Award in 1952. Simak won a Hugo for his novella, "The Big Front Yard." He also won a Hugo for "Way Station" in 1964. Simak was a big wheel in the science fiction world back then. <br/><br/>So again, I ask. why is he forgotten? I have combed the shelves of used book shops, and Simak's books are tough to find. I don't know if this means that collectors tended to hoard Simak's books, or if it means that people commonly th</span>
  <span id="freeText7421789750492913166" style="display:none">Why is Clifford Simak virtually a forgotten writer?<br /><br />"City" won the International Fantasy Award in 1952. Simak won a Hugo for his novella, "The Big Front Yard." He also won a Hugo for "Way Station" in 1964. Simak was a big wheel in the science fiction world back then. <br /><br />So again, I ask. why is he forgotten? I have combed the shelves of used book shops, and Simak's books are tough to find. I don't know if this means that collectors tended to hoard Simak's books, or if it means that people commonly threw them out when they finished reading them.<br /><br />"City" is a fascinating book. I'm sure that many readers today would find it lacking in interesting human characters; lacking in a compelling plot; lacking female characters; and disappointingly undramatic. It's certainly not a novel in the conventional sense. (But then, neither was Bradbury's "Martian Chronicles.")<br /><br />Nevertheless, Simak's book is both quaint and brilliant. For example, the most consistent and memorable character throughout the book is a robot waiter called Jenkins, who, over many generations, serves whisky to a family of humans called the Websters. The overarching premise of the book is that humans eventually find a better life on Jupiter after an experimental team converts their bodies into another species. When the general population discovers that this paradisical life is available through such conversion, most of them abandon Earth. Earth is thus left in the hands of engineered dogs and robots, who begin to build their own pacifist cultures. Man (Simaks term; we'd say "humans" today, to put it in a sex-neutral way) becomes a forgotten species or a dimly remembered, mythical creature that adult dogs tell stories about to their pups before bed. Dogs sit around the fire and debate whether humans and cities ever dominated the earth in the remote past, as the myths relate.<br /><br />A small band of survivalist humans remains at large. Before long, the dogs and robots discover that, despite their efforts to purge the band of their propensity for violence, human blood-lust is unstoppable. So the dogs and robots kindly export the humans to another world. <br /><br />"City" was originally a loosely connected collection of stories, published at different times in Astounding Science fiction (mostly during the "Golden Age" of sf literature). Simak cemented these stories together with some interlinear commentary by dog scholars, in order to sell them as a single, book-length work. The interwoven doggish commentary is written in a mock-scholarly tone, which I found very amusing. <br /><br />I have perused some of the reviews on Goodreads, and many people criticized "City" for painting an unrealistic picture of the future (especially on account of the unrealistic premise that humans could engineer dogs so that they might read and talk).<br /><br />It irritates me when people judge science fiction based upon whether or not the author was able to predict the future. Also, it irritates me when people expect science fiction to be strictly "scientific." <br /><br />For me, the point of science fiction is to explore what it means to be human. That exploration is accomplished by imagining a world that is quite different from the one we live in, or contains sentient beings with a non-human perspective. It shouldn't matter whether the author's world is completely believable or not. What should matter is whether or not the author uses that world and its beings to shed light on the human condition. <br /><br />By putting humans in an other-worldly context, the writer is able to speculate about how humans would adapt or fail to adapt to that other world. The writer is thus able to expose aspects humanity that might not otherwise come to light, or advance a thesis on the nature of humanity.<br /><br />In a way, I found "City" to be absurd. But by this I don't mean inferior. I mean absurd in the way Kafka's stories are absurd. And this is really how "City" should be read. Not as an attempt to predict the future, but more as an attempt to explore what it means to be human in a playful, non-serious,imaginative, and absurdist way. There is much beauty in Simak's writing, too. Despite his science fiction themes, he lovingly lingers on detailed, pastoral descriptions. <br /><br />Whether or not you love this book, you won't ever read another one quite like it! </span>
  <a data-text-id="7421789750492913166" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_6998598" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6998598" />
    <a title="Christy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/248667-christy"><img alt="Christy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1550952681p2/248667.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6998598?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christy" href="/user/show/248667-christy">Christy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/248667-christy?shelf=science-fiction-and-fantasy">science-fiction-and-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/248667-christy?shelf=readinglist2-sf">readinglist2-sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/248667-christy?shelf=20th-century-american-lit">20th-century-american-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/248667-christy?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/248667-christy?shelf=want-to-reread">want-to-reread</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6998598" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6340036423087083023">I'd read one of the stories in this book before, "Desertion," and loved it. I still think I love that story best, but the whole book is definitely worth reading. In fact, this is one book that I would love to teach, for several reasons.<br/><br/>1. It's a fun read, with some interesting conceits (a future Doggish society [made up of a race of intelligent speaking dogs], space travel, a society of ants, etc.)<br/>2. It demands close reading skills, not just in the stories themselves but in the Doggish commentar</span>
  <span id="freeText6340036423087083023" style="display:none">I'd read one of the stories in this book before, "Desertion," and loved it. I still think I love that story best, but the whole book is definitely worth reading. In fact, this is one book that I would love to teach, for several reasons.<br /><br />1. It's a fun read, with some interesting conceits (a future Doggish society [made up of a race of intelligent speaking dogs], space travel, a society of ants, etc.)<br />2. It demands close reading skills, not just in the stories themselves but in the Doggish commentary on those stories, which reveal a great deal about the future Doggish culture as well as providing some incisive critiques of humanity.<br />3. It raises several really fascinating questions: What counts as intelligence? What is the ultimate value and worth of humanity? How is humanity defined? Is progress worth more than happiness, or is progress necessary for happiness? Is violence inherent within humanity? How intelligent are animals? What would happen if animals had humanly recognizable intelligence and society? What are the possibilities of a "Brotherhood of Beasts," a recognition of connection and commonality across species lines? And is a truly nonviolent world possible? Is it really "better that one should lose a world than go back to killing" (252)? <br /><br />Simak himself says that this book was "written out of disillusion" (1) after World War II. He says, "<i>City</i> was written not as a protest (for what good would protest do?) but as a seeking after a fantasy world that would serve as a counterbalance to the brutality through which the world was passing" (2). He goes on to recognize that he "peopled the fantasy world with dogs and robots because [he] could see little hope of mankind arriving at such a world" (2). I'm not sure I agree with the cynicism of this statement or with <i>City</i>'s insistence that violence is an unavoidable part of human nature, but I do agree that the humankind of the 20th century deserves to be indicted for its behavior. Simak argues in his introduction to the book (from which I have been quoting) that humanity is beyond saving and, further developing this argument, creates in this book a world in which humankind disappears, leaving a more peaceful, kind, and empathic world of Dogs and Robots; I would argue, on the other hand, that Simak's creation of this world shows the possibilities of nonviolence, even for humans, because it shows through this contrast what changes would need to be made to have this kind of world. The Doggish commentator on the stories asks, "If Man had taken a different path, might he not, in time to come, have been as great as Dog?" (146). Despite the violence of the 20th century (and the opening of the 21st) and despite Simak's loss of faith in humanity, I believe there is still a chance for us to take a different path. And the kind of criticism of humanity levied by Simak is an important part of the process involved in finding that path.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6340036423087083023" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_394080220" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/394080220" />
    <a title="Nicky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/223837-nicky"><img alt="Nicky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308224598p2/223837.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/394080220?book_show_action=true">Aug 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicky" href="/user/show/223837-nicky">Nicky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780575105232" href="/book/show/10812222-city">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer394080220" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5508678312108948243">I really wouldn't attempt to read City as speculative fiction, despite the opening stories and the fact that there's space travel and alternate dimensions. After I saw the reactions of group members to it, I thought I wasn't going to get on with it at all -- totally unscientific, only one or two female characters even <i>mentioned</i>, etc.<br/><br/>But then I started reading and the scholarly notes really tickled me. I've read them before, in a sense, in every book that attempts to piece together whether King A</span>
  <span id="freeText5508678312108948243" style="display:none">I really wouldn't attempt to read City as speculative fiction, despite the opening stories and the fact that there's space travel and alternate dimensions. After I saw the reactions of group members to it, I thought I wasn't going to get on with it at all -- totally unscientific, only one or two female characters even <i>mentioned</i>, etc.<br /><br />But then I started reading and the scholarly notes really tickled me. I've read them before, in a sense, in every book that attempts to piece together whether King Arthur (or any other mythical/legendary figure) really existed. I managed to read it then as a myth, as a cleverly constructed series of stories creating a myth-that-might-have-been. Almost a fable (which came to me when the notes made a reference to Aesop). It's a fable of what could happen if we took men out of the equation, and links up with The Book of Merlyn which I reread only last night -- is there something inherent in men that makes us act the way we do? <br /><br />(It and T.H. White's Arthurian stories weren't written that far apart in time. Is it too late for me to write a dissertation on the preoccupations of those decades and take City and The Once and Future King as my primary texts? I'm sure there are others. It's probably been done, though. Striking that they both used ants and dogs, though probably coincidence -- we have very firm ideas of what ants and dogs are like, what they do, and I think they both used a common image.)<br /><br />Anyway, it's not a gripping story with a narrative that pushes you forward. I read it with more a gentle curiosity, and it responds well to that.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5508678312108948243" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1762873982" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1762873982" />
    <a title="Rachel (Kalanadi)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17810287-rachel-kalanadi"><img alt="Rachel (Kalanadi)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574017820p2/17810287.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1762873982?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel (Kalanadi)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel (Kalanadi)" href="/user/show/17810287-rachel-kalanadi">Rachel (Kalanadi)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780575105232" href="/book/show/10812222-city">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17810287-rachel-kalanadi?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1762873982" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15669884283444361580">A really good read. I like the framework used to stitch the stories together, with Doggish academics arguing about whether Man existed or not. The one reason it never truly got off the ground for me is that the science is so clearly wrong and odd, and even though I certainly know this is old and Simak writes very pastoral sci fi, I could not turn off the questioning part of my brain that constantly cried "but that makes no sense!" But it was still good.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3074163168" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3074163168" />
    <a title="J.M. Hushour" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17463213-j-m-hushour"><img alt="J.M. Hushour" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442964894p2/17463213.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3074163168?book_show_action=true">Dec 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.M. Hushour" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.M. Hushour" href="/user/show/17463213-j-m-hushour">J.M. Hushour</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780575105232" href="/book/show/10812222-city">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3074163168" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9973717070695723969">"Man gave up trying. Man enjoyed himself. Human achievement became a zero factor and human life a senseless paradise."<br/><br/>Another excellent, mid-century sci-fi novel noteworthy for its sheer alienness. I was reminded of Clement's <i>Mission of Gravity</i> that I read recently. This is very different from Simak's other book I also read recently, <i>Way Station</i>. The latter was downhome, unsettling first contact weirdness, in a way, whereas this one is far more alien, far-reaching, and even eerie sometimes.<br/>Like </span>
  <span id="freeText9973717070695723969" style="display:none">"Man gave up trying. Man enjoyed himself. Human achievement became a zero factor and human life a senseless paradise."<br /><br />Another excellent, mid-century sci-fi novel noteworthy for its sheer alienness. I was reminded of Clement's <i>Mission of Gravity</i> that I read recently. This is very different from Simak's other book I also read recently, <i>Way Station</i>. The latter was downhome, unsettling first contact weirdness, in a way, whereas this one is far more alien, far-reaching, and even eerie sometimes.<br />Like all good stories, <i>City</i> is about the death of an institution, here the titular institution. In the late 20th century (the book is from 1952), humans decide that cities make too good a target so they retreat into the countryside with robot servants and basically live needless, carefree lives. War ends. All the evils of social accretion are gone. Except folks get a little too apathetic. Mutants arise who are quietly all-powerful and even more apathetic. Humans biomechanically engineer Dogs (the tellers of the myths the story takes the form of) and the Dogs try to make good where humans didn't.<br />Then it gets weird. There are inner worlds populated by wraith-like creatures, most humans opt to transform themselves into weird monsters on Jupiter, and a 7000-year old robot thinks the Ants are getting a little too big for their britches.<br /></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Samuel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Samuel" href="/user/show/5354906-samuel">Samuel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer232988760" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5347703037328940201">Biologically, this book is absurd: quasi-robotic intelligent dogs, hyper-evolved progressive rural humans with an intelligence seemingly gained from nothing whatsoever, a race of ants experiencing socio-economic and industrial revolutions, evolution stemming from surgery; to name but a few. Philosophically, it's broken and contradictory to the point of frustration; economically null, politically ridiculous and simply completely ignorant of the <i>science</i> in science-fiction. That this collection of </span>
  <span id="freeText5347703037328940201" style="display:none">Biologically, this book is absurd: quasi-robotic intelligent dogs, hyper-evolved progressive rural humans with an intelligence seemingly gained from nothing whatsoever, a race of ants experiencing socio-economic and industrial revolutions, evolution stemming from surgery; to name but a few. Philosophically, it's broken and contradictory to the point of frustration; economically null, politically ridiculous and simply completely ignorant of the <i>science</i> in science-fiction. That this collection of linked short stories has behind it the positive words of both Asimov and Heinlein baffles me. All I can assume is that I'm about two generations late to the party, and that the party wasn't what I was expecting at all. The abandonment and disbanding of the globe's cities was such an exciting idea, it's a shame it turned into see-through futurism and tacky fantasy.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_624992346" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/624992346" />
    <a title="Chris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7950244-chris"><img alt="Chris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480103288p2/7950244.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/624992346?book_show_action=true">May 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris" href="/user/show/7950244-chris">Chris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/25855078-city">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7950244-chris?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7950244-chris?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer624992346" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13374926323807425844">Clifford Simak's fame has waned in the years after his death, and he never was one of the more well-known or popular SF authors to begin with. He broke onto the SF scene in 1944 with a series of semi-linked short stories and novellas, a future-history that took humanity out of its near-future cites, into star-studded galaxies, even beyond mere homo sapiens. He continued writing them through 1947, then published one final tale in 1951, at which point they were joined together and sold as the fixu</span>
  <span id="freeText13374926323807425844" style="display:none">Clifford Simak's fame has waned in the years after his death, and he never was one of the more well-known or popular SF authors to begin with. He broke onto the SF scene in 1944 with a series of semi-linked short stories and novellas, a future-history that took humanity out of its near-future cites, into star-studded galaxies, even beyond mere homo sapiens. He continued writing them through 1947, then published one final tale in 1951, at which point they were joined together and sold as the fixup novel City. (One more was added in 1971 in a volume honoring editor John W. Campbell, who had published all but one of the others in Astounding Science Fiction.) It remains something of a minor classic to this day, having made quite an impact in introducing Simak's pastoral and mournful themes to science fiction.<br /><br />City's frame story comes in the form of academic notes left by humanity's successor, intelligent dogs uplifted by a man named Webster. Each of City's stories are part of the dogs' oral history, myths passed down from generation to generation of a race called humans---whose existence is hotly contested. And each of the stories follows some humans of the Webster family, telling of the Websters' roles across human history, both successes and failures. Readers who see SF as an attempt at prediction/prophecy will be disappointed with the direction Simak takes his future history; those of us more interested in the story's insights or context have a wealth of material at hand.<br /><br />"City" starts off strong with its pastoral, mournful atmosphere; in a predicted post-War future, where cheap atomic-powered aircraft sees decentralized population centers end the Cold War---why live in a city when you can live in the beautiful country and commute in a ten-minute plane ride? "City" imagines the decline of the city, with the Webster family one of the last inhabitants of a dying town; it's followed up by "Huddling Place," one of the most effective stories in the collection. "Huddling Place" deals with the fallout of this shift---the Webster of the next generation is a brilliant surgeon with an acute case of agoraphobia; despite the pastoral beginnings, Simak seems to be implying that the fiction flight to rural life had unintended consequences. (A few other humans become mutants with strange abilities, possibly also due to the societal shift.) The issue is compacted by a summons to save the live of one of his friends, a Martian philosopher, and the story is a decent psychological tale as Webster struggles to overcome his fears.<br /><br />The science and logic behind Simak's tales often require a leap of faith; I'm not sure I agree that technology would lead to shrinking urban centers, though it's a nice utopian idea and makes for a fascinating story. More often than not I find them a success of pathos over logos---also, both a contrast to other SF of the era, and direct commentary on its point in time. "Desertion," the other stand-out story in the book, sees a Jupiter survey mission open a gateway to transcendence, where humans remake themselves anew and flee into their posthuman future. It happens to be the first of these stories that Simak wrote (in 1943, though it was published fourth), a direct reaction to early reports from Europe about the Holocaust---actually per Simak, the entire book "was written in a revulsion against mass killing and as a protest against war." There's something poignant about "Desertion," where an author so disillusioned with humanity's propensity for violence reshapes this nature by way of fiction. For me, knowing the historical context amplifies the utopian fantasy themes.<br /><br />The stories continue to develop and escalate along those lines; robotics and artificial intelligence are developed, and uplifted man's best friend to sentience. By the time of the sixth story "Hobbies," there is only one city left, Geneva, in which the few listless humans who won't leave for Jovian transcendence struggle to find a purpose---facing an apocalypse of pastoral isolation. Humankind drifts off into the ether, taking the "long sleep" of cryogenics or becoming more-than-human on Jupiter, leaving Earth behind for its servitors---its creations, the uplifted dogs and the robots, left to guard and guide the world in humanity's absence. And despite some wraithlike alien presence only the dogs can detect, the dogs develop their own utopia free of violence and want. Yet they too are fallible, with exactly the opposite flaws compared to the humans. Each of the stories is outlined by notes from doggish scholars, and the dogs' role in the tales increases as humanity's fades.<br /><br />If commentary by way of sentient dogs sounds a bit ridiculous or cheesy, note that there is (or at least was) a firm debate on whether they added or subtracted from the novel. On the one hand, the stories stand on their own fairly well, while the doggish commentary often includes twee jokes, and ham-handedly ram home points that the stories were quite capable of making. On the other hand, the dogs' analysis of each story brings out more of Simak's points and themes; he uses them to isolate and outline the philosophical issues his stories raise---in between a few bad jokes (the one where dog scholars believe "woman" and "wife" to be interchangeable with "female" felt painfully antiquated). The idea of a sentient, uplifted dog doesn't concern me, though to be honest I think the stories are more poignant without the introductions.<br /><br />Because, you see, the stories aren't about the dogs---despite how the novel may appear. Even when all that is left is doggish, the dogs are used to display the human absence. City is about humanity, how it changes over time and reacts to the fictional situations Simak places it in. The book raises some fascinating questions about progress and intelligence and human nature. Simak was ahead of the curve in penning stories that were often more philosophical and cerebral, especially compared to neighboring stories in Astounding---Asimov's robot mysteries, Kuttner's robot puzzles, and an onslaught of Hard SF: can-do engineers overcoming obstacles and adventures in rigorous physics speculation.<br /><br />City demands that its readers accept its conceits---with the dogs, with the gaps in logic and science, and with some of its more inventive and wild themes (malevolent mutant psychics, for one). If you can accept that imaginative vision knowing it is not real but a dream, you'll find some of the best SF stories from the 1940s. Its pages are full of potent and insightful ideas, the novel being a subtle-but-deep examination of multiple themes told using Simak's soft touch and pastoral ambiance. It's also deceptively good, and remains a more than worthy read---it remains a classic of science fiction, underrated and forgotten though it is.<br /><br />I recieved an eARC from NetGalley and Open Road Media in exchange for this open and honest review. <a href="https://yellowedandcreased.wordpress.com/2015/07/29/city-clifford-simak/" rel="nofollow noopener">Review, and more classic SF reviews, on my blog.</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="13374926323807425844" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Simon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1071724-simon"><img alt="Simon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1242398665p2/1071724.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/108513120?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simon" href="/user/show/1071724-simon">Simon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780722178577" href="/book/show/1198130.City">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1071724-simon?shelf=sf-masterworks">sf-masterworks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1071724-simon?shelf=sf">sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer108513120" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8413083819409143896">I have to say that this was quite a disappointment for me and not what I was expecting after reading the excellent <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/190999.Way_Station' title='Way Station by Clifford D. Simak' rel='nofollow noopener'>Way Station</a>.<br/><br/>"City" is basically a chronicle of mankind's demise, usually involving characters who are decendents of the Webster family who invariably end up involved in pivotal events in our future history. No single event or catastrophy here, rather it is a gradual decline. And the reasons are more social, cultural and psycological than anything else. <br/><br/>This is actually a collection </span>
  <span id="freeText8413083819409143896" style="display:none">I have to say that this was quite a disappointment for me and not what I was expecting after reading the excellent <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/190999.Way_Station" title="Way Station by Clifford D. Simak" rel="nofollow noopener">Way Station</a>.<br /><br />"City" is basically a chronicle of mankind's demise, usually involving characters who are decendents of the Webster family who invariably end up involved in pivotal events in our future history. No single event or catastrophy here, rather it is a gradual decline. And the reasons are more social, cultural and psycological than anything else. <br /><br />This is actually a collection of interelated stories, depicted as a set of legends that dogs tell each other around the camp fire. Yes, for dogs have inherited the earth and mankind is now no more than a myth disbelieved by all except their most crackpot scholars. There is a fictitious editorial introduction to each story, whom we are to think of as a dog, that briefly expounds various canine interpretations of the stories. Daft as this might sound, these parts were actually my favourite parts of the book.<br /><br />Various things let this book down in my opinion. Firstly it just hasn't dated very well. Both in terms of the ideas and the dialogue. Okay, it was first published in 1951 but it must have seemed very far fetched even then. The ideas become more wild and ludicrous as we get further into the future until we end up in the realms of pure fantasy. The character's usually sounds like hillbillies, even when it's the animals who are talking (yes animals aquire a hereditary ability to talk through vivisection!)<br /><br />The above might well have been largely forgiven but for the fact that the story lines are almost completely devoid of narative tension. Occaisionally I thought a story started out interestingly only to skirt around the moments that would help build and maintain the reader's interest to needlessly shift focus elsewhere (like a badly edited movie). Ultimately, it left me largely uninterested in the overall story arc and was a bit of a struggle to finish.<br /><br />Many others seem to be quite fond of this book so perhaps I am being unkind but for me the flaws just kept getting in the way of my getting much out of it. And it's left me not to keep to try anything else by Simak.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8413083819409143896" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_303144320" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/303144320" />
    <a title="Denis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2298109-denis"><img alt="Denis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505535394p2/2298109.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/303144320?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Denis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Denis" href="/user/show/2298109-denis">Denis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/8683844-city">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2298109-denis?shelf=softcover">softcover</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer303144320" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4038139351124224274">This is a classic of the 'Golden Age' fix-up novel - A.E. van Vogt was not the only author to capitalize on repackage a series of earlier short stories into novel form. Asimov's "I Robot" is another fine example, for instance.<br/><br/>The complete "City" consists of: <br/>· City · nv Astounding May 1944<br/>· Huddling Place · ss Astounding Jul 1944<br/>· Census · nv Astounding Sep 1944<br/>· Desertion · ss Astounding Nov 1944<br/>· Paradise · ss Astounding Jun 1946<br/>· Hobbies · nv Astounding Nov 1946<br/>· Aesop · nv Astounding Dec 194</span>
  <span id="freeText4038139351124224274" style="display:none">This is a classic of the 'Golden Age' fix-up novel - A.E. van Vogt was not the only author to capitalize on repackage a series of earlier short stories into novel form. Asimov's "I Robot" is another fine example, for instance.<br /><br />The complete "City" consists of: <br />· City · nv Astounding May 1944<br />· Huddling Place · ss Astounding Jul 1944<br />· Census · nv Astounding Sep 1944<br />· Desertion · ss Astounding Nov 1944<br />· Paradise · ss Astounding Jun 1946<br />· Hobbies · nv Astounding Nov 1946<br />· Aesop · nv Astounding Dec 1947<br />· The Simple Way [The Trouble with Ants] · nv Fantastic Adventures Jan 1951<br />· Author’s Note · is<br />· Epilog · ss Astounding, ed. Harry Harrison, Random, 1973<br /><br />(Mine was a an old 1952 ACE paperback, so the 1973 segment "Epilog" had not yet been written - I got to read that later on.)<br /><br />Simak's writing tends to be rather quiet and introspective. Often, the setting is rural. It is, for the most part, simple clear story telling. Perhaps this economy has to do with he being a career newspaper man until he retired in the mid seventies.<br /><br />This novel, upon description, seems outrageous: Over time, human population diminishes on this Earth as most folks chose to move away from large cities into the country side, until finally there is no one left as they have moved off planet all together, leaving talking dogs and robots behind... That sounds reasonable enough wouldn't you agree?<br /><br />Out of an armful of novels and short stories I have read by Simak so far, this will always be a favourite. It is far from scientific and plausible in anyway that an Asimov or Clark, Pohl, and so on, story is, but rather, it has the charm more like, say, a Bradbury story might.<br /><br />If, like myself, you are on a mission to explore all of the major classic authors rooted in the golden age of scifi, and you haven't yet come across Simak, then you have, so far, omitted one of the most unique, though provoking and pleasant reads of the genre. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4038139351124224274" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_192339825" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/192339825" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/554401-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1233528349p2/554401.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/192339825?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark" href="/user/show/554401-mark">Mark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780575105232" href="/book/show/10812222-city">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer192339825" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9613856838426380031">Clifford/Cliff Simak is an author I first came to when I was a teenager in the late 1970’s and early 1980’s. At first I wasn’t sure – it wasn’t spaceships and action, but instead a much more subtler and gentle SF. (Mark Charan Newton has since referred to it as ‘rural SF’, which sorta works.) Instead of Star Wars whizz-bang action, we have pastoral introspection, Waltons-style homily and self-depreciating humour.<br/><br/>And in City in particular we have robots, ants and dogs.<br/> <br/>To my younger self, City wa</span>
  <span id="freeText9613856838426380031" style="display:none">Clifford/Cliff Simak is an author I first came to when I was a teenager in the late 1970’s and early 1980’s. At first I wasn’t sure – it wasn’t spaceships and action, but instead a much more subtler and gentle SF. (Mark Charan Newton has since referred to it as ‘rural SF’, which sorta works.) Instead of Star Wars whizz-bang action, we have pastoral introspection, Waltons-style homily and self-depreciating humour.<br /><br />And in City in particular we have robots, ants and dogs.<br /> <br />To my younger self, City was a bit of a puzzle on first reading. So I read it again. And have a few times since. These days, thirty-odd years on, City is one of my Simak favourites: more than that, it is one of my all-time favourites. And yet I still really don’t know why.<br /> <br /><i>‘These are the stories that the Dogs tell when the fires burn high and the wind is from the north. Then each family circle gathers at the hearthstone and the pups sit silently and listen and when the story’s done they ask many questions:<br />“What is Man?” they’ll ask.<br />Or perhaps: “What is a city?”<br />Or: “What is a war?” ‘ (Page 1.)</i><br /><br />Simply, it is a set of eight interconnected stories (or in some cases, nine, with an extra tale, Epilog, written in 1973 and added in the 1980’s. Here it is not included, sadly). <br /><br />The First Tale sets up the other stories by telling of Gramp Stevens, one of the few people left behind in the decaying cities of America. Gramp decides to stay but watches as his neighbours, faced with relatively unlimited space and easy to gain foodstuffs, leave the cities and move to live in small rural enclaves, self-sufficiently producing what they need for their communities. <br /><br />The Second Tale, also called The Huddling Place, tells of the psychological effects of this change, where people, personified by the Webster Family, and comfortable in their own surroundings, become increasingly isolated and agoraphobic. Jerome A. Webster is the only human able to operate on Martian philosopher Juwain, his close acquaintance. The only problem is that to save his friend has to leave his house and travel to Mars. <br /><br />The third tale, Census, tells of what happens to the Websters in the next generation, with the arrival to their home of Richard Grant, an enumerator. We are introduced to dogs that can talk and also to Joe, a mutant, one of many, whose lifespan lasts for hundreds of years and seems to be watching and supporting human evolution.<br /> <br />From the Third Tale onwards we have the arrival and increasing importance of the dogs and follow the lives of the human descendants of the Webster family, watched over by near-immortal mutants, and their ever-faithful robots (especially Jenkins, the robot butler) and dogs. Descended from the Webster family’s pet hound, the tales of City are told by dogs as the stories are passed down from generation to generation, holding the torch long after their original purpose has gone. For their future generations they tell folktales of what happened to the humans, and why the dogs and robots still continue to do what they do.<br /><br />The Fourth and Fifth Tales change the focus from Earth by looking at the humans who have left the Earth. The Fourth tale begins on Jupiter, where in order to survive the extreme conditions there, humans have to be re-engineered to survive. There is a problem in that those adapted (known as ‘Lopers’) who do so have not returned to the base. Kent Fowler and his faithful dog Towser are the next to investigate, whose actions lead to repercussions for the human race. We return to Earth for the conclusion of this in the Fifth Tale. <br /><br />By the Sixth Tale Jon Webster tells of how the Earth has become mechanised, with robots producing all that is required by humans, with no need to work, shop, or farm, no government and no religion. It is clear that things are not good in this idyll however, and many of the few residents who remain take ‘The Sleep’, suspended animation until times are better. Jon Webster spends twenty years writing a book about the city of Geneva, the last city on Earth, but by doing so tells of Earth’s decline of civilisation until he makes a life-changing decision. <br /><br />Tale Seven, Aesop, tells of robot Jenkins, now seven thousand years old and his mentoring of the dogs and humans to ensure their survival in the future. We also have the rediscovery of a wonderful invention which explains some things and creates future opportunities.<br />By Tale Eight the dogs and robots are ultimately left as guardians by the humans on Earth, though long-since forgotten, with the planet seemingly being taken over by the ants.<br /><br />This is a grand scale of a tale, but not without its weaknesses.<br /><br />Frankly, from the perspective of 2011, it is slowly paced and quite dated in its style. We have servant butlers, loyal to a system of servitude. It is also quite noticeable that this is clearly a man’s world, with women significantly absent from the majority of the tales. <br />Stylistically too there are issues. There’s a bit of repetition as the reader is reminded of what happened in the previous tales: useful when the stories are told months apart, less necessary when it is a matter of a few pages. The dialogue at times is quite stilted for the 21st century. <br /><br />However, it is also sad, melancholy, wistful and one of those series of collective tales that just keeps resurfacing in the reader’s memory. If you can live with the dated aspects, there’s a lot to enjoy here.<br /><br />For the record, I like dogs. It would be easy to say that my love of the book is simply because of that, that it shows Man(kind) living in harmony with his faithful companion. Yet it is more than that. City shows Simak’s deceptively difficult authorial trick of telling fireside tales in a low-key, comfortable way, that stay with you long after you’ve read them. And are worth rereading: again. There are other Simak tales that are as good, if not better, yet this one still remains in my memory. He is an underrated writer that is worthy of reassessment. And to my mind, City still resonates as a worthy read.<br /><br />Recommended.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9613856838426380031" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_967304234" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/967304234" />
    <a title="Monica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4739465-monica"><img alt="Monica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577896647p2/4739465.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/967304234?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Monica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Monica" href="/user/show/4739465-monica">Monica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780441106257" href="/book/show/898828.City">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=scifi-earth">scifi-earth</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=meta-ethics-philosophy-spiritual">meta-ethics-philosophy-spiritual</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=scifi-dystopia">scifi-dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=pub_1950">pub_1950</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=notasgoodasexpected">notasgoodasexpected</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=did-not-like">did-not-like</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer967304234" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14093489637760393774">This book was a strange one. A odd concoction of philosophy and futility. Essentially, Simak was disillusioned with mankind after the destruction with the atomic bomb in Japan. He comes to what I find a very strange conclusion that city living is the cause of the bulk of human antisocial and violent behavior. He advocates much smaller communities and talks of how the invention of the telephone negates the need to live close to where one works. I got the impression that he was a bit of a recluse.</span>
  <span id="freeText14093489637760393774" style="display:none">This book was a strange one. A odd concoction of philosophy and futility. Essentially, Simak was disillusioned with mankind after the destruction with the atomic bomb in Japan. He comes to what I find a very strange conclusion that city living is the cause of the bulk of human antisocial and violent behavior. He advocates much smaller communities and talks of how the invention of the telephone negates the need to live close to where one works. I got the impression that he was a bit of a recluse. His cynicism permeates the entire book. This book consists of 8 short stories chronologically interconnected in series. Mankind in its narcissistic splendor has simply lost the will to continue on. There is no meaning in life, no challenges. Most of mankind goes to Jupiter and transforms into another life form which harbors the promise of clarity and purpose. A small group of mankind stays behind and put themselves in indefinite hibernation because their lives have no purpose. Mutants have discovered inter-dimensional travel and are no longer on earth. Early on mankind gives dogs the ability to speak. Some how dogs pass on this ability to other animals and animals who do not know of mankind and manage to live together harmoniously without violence. The world the animals create is with the assistance of robots who act as their hands in order to build and farm etc. Insects have also evolved and can't communicate with the animals but can communicate with the robots. Ultimately, the insects inherit the earth.<br /><br />For my taste, the book simply doesn't stand up to the test of time. Lots of the elements make no sense. For example there are wild bands of robots who simply went rogue when the humans left (BTW there is nothing wild about these robots. They do nothing to harm or destroy or disrupt the environment or life. It is unclear what is "wild" about them in the story). The robots in this story (the main robot character Jenkins for example) have emotions, thoughts and fears. Simak projects human characteristics onto robots and then tells you they are different. They are indistinguishable from the humans in the book (with the exception of lifespan). The relationship between humans and robots doesn't resemble man and machine, it resembles master and slave. That context dates the book as well. It is a bit of a peek into the minds of 1950s societal proclivities. Strange and conflicting social biases in my view. To explain briefly, why would it appear to be socially acceptable to give machines free thought and emotions if the intent is to enslave? Jenkins' driving force seemed to be the memory of mankind and to guide the dogs/animals away from the foibles of mankind (ie keeper of the faith). Funny that Simak's imagination didn't go beyond always having some sort of dominating species even amongst the animals. In this story that species is dogs, but ever present is the notion that man is ultimately a species to be worshiped. Also, there is no cataclysmic event that leads the onset of apathy in mankind. Nothing major happened, mankind just faded away. There is a very odd implication that a lost alien philosophy somehow could have reignited purpose for the human race. Yes you read that right. An alien <u>philosophy</u> somehow so strange and sophisticated that only the alien (in this case martians who apparently died out after that story) could conceive of it and yet it would have saved mankind. Just no...<br /><br />I suppose my main disappointment with the book is that I think it was executed poorly. It is a philosophical piece, but Simak really doesn't have anything to say. Simak is in a funk and he's working his way through it by writing an incoherent tale. One gets the impression he had a faithful dog and mused how much better the world would be if dogs ruled. His lens (of course) is focused on his perception of dogs. The actual question of what would a world be like if dogs ruled is not really examined, it is just presumed that all dogs have similar characteristics and temperaments. The book is simplistic and lacks focus. The science is laughable, even when you take into account the level of scientific knowledge in 1952. In short, each tale has some sort of "Deus ex Machina" element to it; from the talking dogs, to the conversion to an alien species, to the inter-dimensional travel, to the rise of mutants, to the renegade robots, to the intelligent ants etc. This is considered a classic, however there are far more prescient and coherent works from the grand masters in the golden age of science fiction. Honestly these older science fiction reads either knock it out of the park or fall so flat it's hard to believe they are beloved. Reduced by one star because the more I think about the novel, the sillier it becomes.<br /><br /><i>almost 2.5 stars</i> </span>
  <a data-text-id="14093489637760393774" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_90591996" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/90591996" />
    <a title="Lesley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/837477-lesley"><img alt="Lesley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1225438657p2/837477.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/90591996?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lesley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lesley" href="/user/show/837477-lesley">Lesley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer90591996" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3630921999662611243">Several months ago, I visited the science fiction museum up in Seattle. It wasn't that impressive to me, like someone's small private collection stretched out to cover a bunch of exhibits. Forrey's house had been more impressive than this place. But I'd had some interest in post-apocalyptic stuff lately, so I paused at the exhibit, and I noticed some books I hadn't read before featured as classics in the genre. Among them were Alas, Babylon and City by Clifford Simak.<br/><br/>I found Alas, Babylon pretty</span>
  <span id="freeText3630921999662611243" style="display:none">Several months ago, I visited the science fiction museum up in Seattle. It wasn't that impressive to me, like someone's small private collection stretched out to cover a bunch of exhibits. Forrey's house had been more impressive than this place. But I'd had some interest in post-apocalyptic stuff lately, so I paused at the exhibit, and I noticed some books I hadn't read before featured as classics in the genre. Among them were Alas, Babylon and City by Clifford Simak.<br /><br />I found Alas, Babylon pretty easily, but City eluded me at local bookstores. In fact, I found myself astonished that none of the stores I'd visited had *any* of Simak's books (much less City, which seems to be regarded as some of his best work). I'd never read him before, but his name had always popped out in my head as one of the classic authors of science fiction. Finally, while visiting some friends in San Diego who were old SF fans, I mentioned this, and they had a copy of the book! They cheerfully lent me it and one other. I cracked open the book, and was glued to it.<br /><br />City is a collection of short stories, originally published in Astounding (the magazine which later became Analog). These stories are book-ended by commentary from dogs, which have now grown to greater intelligence and communicative abilities, and are looking back and debating whether Man truly existed, or if they are merely figments of storytelling used to highlight parables. In the sense that the dogs are looking back, and Man clearly is no longer around, the novel can be called post-apocalyptic I suppose, but it doesn't match the framework in any other manner. There are no big explosions, devastating disasters or biological attacks which wipe out the human race. The stories span tens of thousands of years in time, and as you read them, you see the future of the world gently unfold before you. <br /><br />This novel started making me reconsider my recent book purchasing habits. When contrasted with the book I read before it, The Boy Who Couldn't Sleep, a perfectly fine novel, I realized that some books <b>must</b> be owned, and some books do not. The City <b>must</b> be owned. If you haven't read it, do yourself a favor and look about for a copy (some are expensive, but I have seen inexpensive ones with some digging.)<br /><br />(As a side note, the edition I owned included a story called Epilog, written by Simak twenty years after the original stories were done. Simak included a note in the edition saying that while he felt Epilog had turned out well enough as a story, he wasn't sure how he felt about it being included after the other stories. He was concerned that it took away from the ending. I agree with him - Epilog is a nice story, but the collection as a whole leaves you with a much stronger ending without it. If the version you buy has Epilog, take a moment before you read this story to imagine that the novel ends there, and the impressions you are left with.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="3630921999662611243" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('7K+pY7busq/BIHsQq9LTCCn/+MB0+djgifpWgMTYLlGrl7YxAbH29G4iMFIcKHQP4ZO2deSKLmNgfVaBM+Lyzw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

Fifty-two years later, this horrifying story is a science fiction classic, touted by The Times (London) as having “all the reality of a vividly realized nightmare.”\n\nBill Masen, bandages over his wounded eyes, misses the most spectacular meteorite shower England has ever seen. Removing his bandages the next morning, he finds masses of sightless people wandering the city. He soon meets Josella, another lucky person who has retained her sight, and together they leave the city, aware that the safe, familiar world they knew a mere twenty-four hours before is gone forever.\n\nBut to survive in this post-apocalyptic world, one must survive the Triffids, strange plants that years before began appearing all over the world. The Triffids can grow to over seven feet tall, pull their roots from the ground to walk, and kill a man with one quick lash of their poisonous stingers. With society in shambles, they are now poised to prey on humankind. Wyndham chillingly anticipates bio-warfare and mass destruction, fifty years before their realization, in this prescient account of Cold War paranoia.<\/span>\n  <a <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52930337-mai-no-he-ent-s-els-robots"><img alt="Mai no he entés els robots" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1571146302l/52930337._SX98_SY160_.jpg" />

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_52930337'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52930337-mai-no-he-ent-s-els-robots?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Mai no he entés els robots<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/54475.Mike_Resnick?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Mike Resnick<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.21 avg rating &mdash; 14 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published \n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer628804641985106025\">Les quimeres del que ens deporta el futur i del que ens defineix del passat són part de l’essència que ens fa humans, però sovint són les carències més que el que posseïm, el que ens perfila. Els relats de Mike Resnick, que treballa des de la ficció <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText628804641985106025\" style=\"display:none\">Les quimeres del que ens deporta el futur i del que ens defineix del passat són part de l’essència que ens fa humans, però sovint són les carències més que el que posseïm, el que ens perfila. Els relats de Mike Resnick, que treballa des de la ficció sociològica més conjectural, sempre ens sorprenen, esbrinant quines són les nostres carències més pregones, i quines les nostres ambicions més fosques. Això és el que trobareu en aquest recull de relats, cinc contalles que burxen dins de les ombres més fosques de la nostra ànima: «El robot entusiasta» ens mostra com valors petits com l’entusiasme ens poden fer grans; «Viatges amb els meus gats» (guanyador del premi Hugo a millor conte l’any 2005) ens parla de fins a quin punt podem trobar companys de viatge insubstituïbles; «Llimac» ens parla de com és de preuada la companyonia de qui és més aliè; «Un déu prim i pàl·lid», on Mike Resnick torna al world bulding que ha construït sobre les cultures i societats africanes, ens mostra com els déus poden posar en una balança tot allò que valoren dels humans i no sempre sortir gaire ben parats; i, finalment, a «La dona de Frankenstein», finalista del premi Hugo en la categoria de conte curt el 2010, i una petita joia de la meravella que brilla amb dret propi, ens trobem amb una insòlita història d’amor que acaba amb una família molt poc convencional. Resnick no és un autor de ciència-ficció a l’ús: barreja l’especulació més futurista i la narració fantàstica més clàssica en una al·legoria de cap a on va la nostra societat, i també d’on ve. Perquè ambdues coses són definitòries de qui som. El que és segur és que Resnick no deixa indiferent i, per bé o per mal, quan acabes de llegir un dels seus relats, sents que t’ha tocat l’ànima... Deixaràs que el seu dit de narrador burxi dins del teu cor? Mike Diamond Resnick (Chicago, 1942) és un dels autors més prolífics de la ciència-ficció anglosaxona. Ha publicat més de 70 novel·les, gairebé 300 contes i 30 reculls de contes, i ha editat més de 40 antologies. A més, ha guanyat cinc premis Hugo (amb 37 nominacions), un Nebula, un Locus, tres premis Ignotus i tres premis Ictineu. És un autor molt interessat en la cultura africana i això es reflecteix molt en els seus llibres. Entre les seves obres més conegudes hi trobem llibres com Kirinyaga (1988), Santiago: A Mith from a Far Future (1986) o Seven Views of Olduvai Gorge (1995). En aquesta editorial ha publicat el recull de contes Sistemes operatius alternatius (2017).<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"628804641985106025\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return
